<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.15em;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lutherrose.svg"><img alt="Lutherrose.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Lutherrose.svg/16px-Lutherrose.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Lutherrose.svg/24px-Lutherrose.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Lutherrose.svg/32px-Lutherrose.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Lutheranism" title="Portal:Lutheranism">Lutheranism portal</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Lutheranism" title="Template:Lutheranism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Lutheranism" title="Template talk:Lutheranism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Lutheranism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Martin Luther</b>, <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">O.S.A.</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span><span title="/θ/: 'th' in 'thigh'">θ</span><span title="/ər/: 'er' in 'letter'">ər</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <small>German:</small> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈmaɐ̯tiːn ˈlʊtɐ]</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Martin_Luther.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/Martin_Luther.ogg" title="Martin Luther.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>; 10 November 1483 – 18 February 1546) was a German professor of <a href="/wiki/Theology#Christian_theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, composer, priest, and monk,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and a seminal figure in the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a>.</p>
<p>Luther came to reject several teachings and practices of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>. He strongly disputed the Catholic view on <a href="/wiki/Indulgence" title="Indulgence">indulgences</a> that freedom from God's punishment for sin could be purchased with money. Luther proposed an academic discussion of the practice and efficacy of indulgences in his <i><a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a></i> of 1517. His refusal to renounce all of his writings at the demand of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_X" title="Pope Leo X">Pope Leo X</a> in 1520 and the <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor Charles V</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Worms" title="Diet of Worms">Diet of Worms</a> in 1521 resulted in his <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Pope</a> and condemnation as an <a href="/wiki/Outlaw" title="Outlaw">outlaw</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor</a>.</p>
<p>Luther taught that <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> and, consequently, eternal life are not earned by good deeds but are received only as the free gift of God's <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace#Grace_in_the_Protestant_Reformation" title="Divine grace">grace</a> through the believer's <a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Christianity#Lutheranism" title="Faith in Christianity">faith</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus Christ</a> as redeemer from sin. <a href="/wiki/Theology_of_Martin_Luther" title="Theology of Martin Luther">His theology</a> challenged the authority and office of the Pope by teaching that the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">only source</a> of <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divinely</a> revealed knowledge from God<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and opposed <a href="/wiki/Sacerdotalism" title="Sacerdotalism">sacerdotalism</a> by considering <a href="/wiki/Universal_priesthood" title="Universal priesthood">all baptized Christians to be a holy priesthood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Those who identify with these, and all of Luther's wider teachings, are called <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutherans</a>, though Luther insisted on <i>Christian</i> or <i>Evangelical</i> (<i>evangelisch</i>) as the only acceptable names for individuals who professed Christ.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">His translation of the Bible</a> into the German <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> (instead of <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>) made it more accessible to the laity, an event that had a tremendous impact on both the church and German culture. It fostered the development of a standard version of the <a href="/wiki/German_language#Modern_German" title="German language">German language</a>, added several principles to the art of translation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> and influenced the writing of an English translation, the <a href="/wiki/Tyndale_Bible" title="Tyndale Bible">Tyndale Bible</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tyndale_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tyndale-6">[6]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Hymn" title="Hymn">hymns</a> influenced the development of singing in Protestant churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bainton269_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bainton269-7">[7]</a></sup> His marriage to <a href="/wiki/Katharina_von_Bora" title="Katharina von Bora">Katharina von Bora</a>, a former nun, set a model for the practice of <a href="/wiki/Clerical_marriage" title="Clerical marriage">clerical marriage</a>, allowing Protestant <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">clergy</a> to marry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bainton223_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bainton223-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In two of his later works, Luther expressed antagonistic views towards <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, writing that Jewish homes and <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogues</a> should be destroyed, their money confiscated, and liberty curtailed. Condemned by virtually every Lutheran denomination, these statements and their <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_and_antisemitism" title="Martin Luther and antisemitism">influence on antisemitism</a> have contributed to his controversial status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_education">Birth and education</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hans_and_Margarethe_Luther,_by_Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="767" data-file-width="1455" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Hans_and_Margarethe_Luther%2C_by_Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder.jpg/220px-Hans_and_Margarethe_Luther%2C_by_Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Hans_and_Margarethe_Luther%2C_by_Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder.jpg/330px-Hans_and_Margarethe_Luther%2C_by_Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Hans_and_Margarethe_Luther%2C_by_Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder.jpg/440px-Hans_and_Margarethe_Luther%2C_by_Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hans_and_Margarethe_Luther,_by_Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portraits of Hans and Margarethe Luther by <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a>, 1527.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Martin Luther was born to Hans Luder (or Ludher, later Luther)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty1_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty1-10">[10]</a></sup> and his wife Margarethe (née Lindemann) on 10 November 1483 in <a href="/wiki/Eisleben" title="Eisleben">Eisleben</a>, Saxony, then part of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a>. He was baptized the next morning on the feast day of <a href="/wiki/Martin_of_Tours" title="Martin of Tours">St. Martin of Tours</a>. His family moved to <a href="/wiki/Mansfeld" title="Mansfeld">Mansfeld</a> in 1484, where his father was a leaseholder of copper mines and smelters<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brecht3_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brecht3-11">[11]</a></sup> and served as one of four citizen representatives on the local council. Hans Luther was chosen a town councilor in 1492.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty1_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marty1-10">[10]</a></sup> The religious scholar <a href="/wiki/Martin_E._Marty" title="Martin E. Marty">Martin Marty</a> describes Luther's mother as a hard-working woman of "trading-class stock and middling means" and notes that Luther's enemies later wrongly described her as a whore and bath attendant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty1_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marty1-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>He had several brothers and sisters, and is known to have been close to one of them, Jacob.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty3_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty3-13">[13]</a></sup> Hans Luther was ambitious for himself and his family, and he was determined to see Martin, his eldest son, become a lawyer. He sent Martin to Latin schools in Mansfeld, then <a href="/wiki/Magdeburg" title="Magdeburg">Magdeburg</a> in 1497, where he attended a school operated by a <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">lay group</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Brethren_of_the_Common_Life" title="Brethren of the Common Life">Brethren of the Common Life</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eisenach" title="Eisenach">Eisenach</a> in 1498.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RuppEB_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-RuppEB-14">[14]</a></sup> The three schools focused on the so-called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trivium_(education)" title="Trivium (education)">trivium</a>": grammar, rhetoric, and logic. Luther later compared his education there to <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">purgatory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">hell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty2-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1501, at the age of 17, he entered the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Erfurt" title="University of Erfurt">University of Erfurt</a>, which he later described as a beerhouse and whorehouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty4_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty4-16">[16]</a></sup> He was made to wake at four every morning for what has been described as "a day of rote learning and often wearying spiritual exercises."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty4_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marty4-16">[16]</a></sup> He received his master's degree in 1505.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty5_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty5-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder_-_Martin_Luther,_Bust_in_Three-Quarter_View_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2401" data-file-width="1693" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder_-_Martin_Luther%2C_Bust_in_Three-Quarter_View_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder_-_Martin_Luther%2C_Bust_in_Three-Quarter_View_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder_-_Martin_Luther%2C_Bust_in_Three-Quarter_View_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder_-_Martin_Luther%2C_Bust_in_Three-Quarter_View_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder_-_Martin_Luther%2C_Bust_in_Three-Quarter_View_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder_-_Martin_Luther%2C_Bust_in_Three-Quarter_View_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder_-_Martin_Luther,_Bust_in_Three-Quarter_View_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Luther as a friar, with tonsure.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In accordance with his father's wishes, Luther enrolled in law school at the same university that year but dropped out almost immediately, believing that law represented uncertainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty5_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marty5-17">[17]</a></sup> Luther sought assurances about life and was drawn to theology and philosophy, expressing particular interest in <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Biel" title="Gabriel Biel">Gabriel Biel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty5_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marty5-17">[17]</a></sup> He was deeply influenced by two tutors, <a href="/wiki/Bartholomaeus_Arnoldi" title="Bartholomaeus Arnoldi">Bartholomaeus Arnoldi</a> von Usingen and Jodocus Trutfetter, who taught him to be suspicious of even the greatest thinkers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty5_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marty5-17">[17]</a></sup> and to test everything himself by experience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty6_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty6-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Philosophy proved to be unsatisfying, offering assurance about the use of <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> but none about loving God, which to Luther was more important. Reason could not lead men to God, he felt, and he thereafter developed a love-hate relationship with Aristotle over the latter's emphasis on reason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty6_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marty6-18">[18]</a></sup> For Luther, reason could be used to question men and institutions, but not God. Human beings could learn about God only through divine <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a>, he believed, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scripture" title="Scripture">Scripture</a> therefore became increasingly important to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty6_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marty6-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>He later attributed his decision to an event: on 2 July 1505, he was returning to university on horseback after a trip home. During a thunderstorm, a lightning bolt struck near him. Later telling his father he was terrified of death and divine judgment, he cried out, "Help! <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Anna" title="Saint Anna">Saint Anna</a>, I will become a monk!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brecht48_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brecht48-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> He came to view his cry for help as a vow he could never break. He left law school, sold his books, and entered <a href="/wiki/St._Augustine%27s_Monastery_(Erfurt)" title="St. Augustine's Monastery (Erfurt)">St. Augustine's Monastery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Erfurt" title="Erfurt">Erfurt</a> on 17 July 1505.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> One friend blamed the decision on Luther's sadness over the deaths of two friends. Luther himself seemed saddened by the move. Those who attended a farewell supper walked him to the door of the Black Cloister. "This day you see me, and then, not ever again," he said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty6_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marty6-18">[18]</a></sup> His father was furious over what he saw as a waste of Luther's education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marty7_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marty7-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_and_academic_life">Early and academic life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Martin_Luther_as_an_Augustinian_Monk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6088" data-file-width="4257" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Portrait_of_Martin_Luther_as_an_Augustinian_Monk.jpg/170px-Portrait_of_Martin_Luther_as_an_Augustinian_Monk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Portrait_of_Martin_Luther_as_an_Augustinian_Monk.jpg/255px-Portrait_of_Martin_Luther_as_an_Augustinian_Monk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Portrait_of_Martin_Luther_as_an_Augustinian_Monk.jpg/340px-Portrait_of_Martin_Luther_as_an_Augustinian_Monk.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_Martin_Luther_as_an_Augustinian_Monk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A posthumous portrait of Luther as an <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinian</a> friar.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Luther dedicated himself to the Augustinian order, devoting himself to <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a>, long hours in <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">prayer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimage</a>, and frequent <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">confession</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bainton40_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bainton40-23">[23]</a></sup> Luther described this period of his life as one of deep spiritual despair. He said, "I lost touch with Christ the Savior and Comforter, and made of him the jailer and hangman of my poor soul."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kittelson79_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kittelson79-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johann_von_Staupitz" title="Johann von Staupitz">Johann von Staupitz</a>, his superior, pointed Luther's mind away from continual reflection upon his sins toward the merits of Christ. He taught that true repentance does not involve self-inflicted penances and punishments but rather a change of heart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 3 April 1507, Jerome Scultetus, Bishop of Brandenburg ordained Luther in <a href="/wiki/Erfurt_Cathedral" title="Erfurt Cathedral">Erfurt Cathedral</a>. In 1508, von Staupitz, first dean of the newly founded <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_University_of_Halle-Wittenberg" title="Martin Luther University of Halle-Wittenberg">University of Wittenberg</a>, sent for Luther, to teach <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFroom1948249_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFroom1948249-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bainton44_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bainton44-27">[27]</a></sup> He received a bachelor's degree in Biblical studies on 9 March 1508, and another bachelor's degree in the <i><a href="/wiki/Sentences" title="Sentences">Sentences</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lombard" title="Peter Lombard">Peter Lombard</a> in 1509.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brecht93_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brecht93-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 19 October 1512, he was awarded his <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Theology" title="Doctor of Theology">Doctor of Theology</a> and, on 21 October 1512, was received into the senate of the theological faculty of the University of Wittenberg,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brecht12_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brecht12-29">[29]</a></sup> having succeeded Staupitz as chair of theology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> He spent the rest of his career in this position at the University of Wittenberg.</p>
<p>He was made provincial <a href="/wiki/Vicar" title="Vicar">vicar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saxony" title="Saxony">Saxony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringia</a> by his religious order in 1515. This meant he was to visit and oversee each of eleven monasteries in his province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Start_of_the_Reformation">Start of the Reformation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Protestantism" title="History of Protestantism">History of Protestantism</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Lutheranism" title="History of Lutheranism">History of Lutheranism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lutherstadt_Wittenberg_09-2016_photo06.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3555" data-file-width="3555" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Lutherstadt_Wittenberg_09-2016_photo06.jpg/220px-Lutherstadt_Wittenberg_09-2016_photo06.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Lutherstadt_Wittenberg_09-2016_photo06.jpg/330px-Lutherstadt_Wittenberg_09-2016_photo06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Lutherstadt_Wittenberg_09-2016_photo06.jpg/440px-Lutherstadt_Wittenberg_09-2016_photo06.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lutherstadt_Wittenberg_09-2016_photo06.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Luther's theses are engraved into the door of <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Church,_Wittenberg" title="All Saints' Church, Wittenberg">All Saints' Church, Wittenberg</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> inscription above informs the reader that the original door was destroyed by a fire, and that in 1857, King <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_IV_of_Prussia" title="Frederick William IV of Prussia">Frederick William IV of Prussia</a> ordered a replacement be made.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1516, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Tetzel" title="Johann Tetzel">Johann Tetzel</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominican_friar" title="Dominican friar">Dominican friar</a> and papal commissioner for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indulgences" title="Indulgences">indulgences</a>, was sent to Germany by the Roman Catholic Church to sell indulgences to raise money in order to rebuild <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Basilica" title="St. Peter's Basilica">St. Peter's Basilica</a> in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Tetzel's experiences as a preacher of indulgences, especially between 1503 and 1510, led to his appointment as general commissioner by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albrecht_von_Brandenburg" title="Albrecht von Brandenburg">Albrecht von Brandenburg, Archbishop of Mainz</a>, who, deeply in debt to pay for a large accumulation of benefices, had to contribute a considerable sum toward the rebuilding of St. Peter's Basilica in Rome. Albrecht obtained permission from <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_X" title="Pope Leo X">Pope Leo X</a> to conduct the sale of a special plenary indulgence (i.e., remission of the temporal punishment of sin), half of the proceeds of which Albrecht was to claim to pay the fees of his benefices.</p>
<p>On 31 October 1517, Luther wrote to his bishop, Albrecht von Brandenburg, protesting the sale of indulgences. He enclosed in his letter a copy of his "Disputation of Martin Luther on the Power and Efficacy of Indulgences", which came to be known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Ninety-five_Theses" title="Ninety-five Theses">Ninety-five Theses</a></i>. Hans Hillerbrand writes that Luther had no intention of confronting the church, but saw his disputation as a scholarly objection to church practices, and the tone of the writing is accordingly "searching, rather than doctrinaire."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HillerbrandIndulgences_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-HillerbrandIndulgences-33">[33]</a></sup> Hillerbrand writes that there is nevertheless an undercurrent of challenge in several of the theses, particularly in Thesis 86, which asks: "Why does the pope, whose wealth today is greater than the wealth of the richest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crassus" title="Crassus">Crassus</a>, build the basilica of St. Peter with the money of poor believers rather than with his own money?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HillerbrandIndulgences_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-HillerbrandIndulgences-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther objected to a saying attributed to Johann Tetzel that "As soon as the coin in the coffer rings, the soul from purgatory (also attested as 'into heaven') springs."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> He insisted that, since <a href="/wiki/Forgiveness" title="Forgiveness">forgiveness</a> was God's alone to grant, those who claimed that indulgences <a href="/wiki/Absolution" title="Absolution">absolved</a> buyers from all punishments and granted them <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> were in error. Christians, he said, must not slacken in following Christ on account of such false assurances.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jeorg_Breu_Elder_A_Question_to_a_Mintmaker_c1500.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="527" data-file-width="750" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Jeorg_Breu_Elder_A_Question_to_a_Mintmaker_c1500.png/220px-Jeorg_Breu_Elder_A_Question_to_a_Mintmaker_c1500.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Jeorg_Breu_Elder_A_Question_to_a_Mintmaker_c1500.png/330px-Jeorg_Breu_Elder_A_Question_to_a_Mintmaker_c1500.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Jeorg_Breu_Elder_A_Question_to_a_Mintmaker_c1500.png/440px-Jeorg_Breu_Elder_A_Question_to_a_Mintmaker_c1500.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jeorg_Breu_Elder_A_Question_to_a_Mintmaker_c1500.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Catholic <a href="/wiki/Indulgence" title="Indulgence">sale of indulgences</a> shown in <i>A Question to a Mintmaker</i>, woodcut by <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6rg_Breu_the_Elder" title="Jörg Breu the Elder">Jörg Breu the Elder</a> of Augsburg, ca. 1530.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, this oft-quoted saying of Tetzel was by no means representative of contemporary Catholic teaching on indulgences, but rather a reflection of Tetzel's capacity to exaggerate. Yet, if Tetzel overstated the matter in regard to indulgences for the <i>dead</i>, his teaching on indulgences for the <i>living</i> was in line with Catholic dogma of the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to one account, Luther nailed his <i>Ninety-five Theses</i> to the door of <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Church,_Wittenberg" title="All Saints' Church, Wittenberg">All Saints' Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wittenberg" title="Wittenberg">Wittenberg</a> on 31 October 1517. Scholars Walter Krämer, Götz Trenkler, Gerhard Ritter, and Gerhard Prause contend that the story of the posting on the door, even though it has settled as one of the pillars of history, has little foundation in truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kr.C3.A4mer_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kr.C3.A4mer-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritter_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ritter-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prause_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prause-38">[38]</a></sup> The story is based on comments made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a>, though it is thought that he was not in Wittenberg at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Latin <i>Theses</i> were printed in several locations in Germany in 1517. In January 1518 friends of Luther translated the <i>Ninety-five Theses</i> from Latin into German.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brecht204_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brecht204-40">[40]</a></sup> Within two weeks, copies of the theses had spread throughout Germany; within two months, they had spread throughout Europe.</p>
<p>Luther's writings circulated widely, reaching France, England, and Italy as early as 1519. Students thronged to Wittenberg to hear Luther speak. He published a short commentary on <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Galatians" title="Epistle to the Galatians">Galatians</a> and his <i>Work on the Psalms</i>. This early part of Luther's career was one of his most creative and productive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Three of his best-known works were published in 1520: <i><a href="/wiki/To_the_Christian_Nobility_of_the_German_Nation" title="To the Christian Nobility of the German Nation">To the Christian Nobility of the German Nation</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Babylonian_Captivity_of_the_Church" title="On the Babylonian Captivity of the Church">On the Babylonian Captivity of the Church</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Freedom_of_a_Christian" title="On the Freedom of a Christian">On the Freedom of a Christian</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Justification_by_faith_alone">Justification by faith alone</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sola_fide" title="Sola fide">Sola fide</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luther_at_Erfurt_-_Justification_by_Faith.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="370" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Luther_at_Erfurt_-_Justification_by_Faith.jpg/170px-Luther_at_Erfurt_-_Justification_by_Faith.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Luther_at_Erfurt_-_Justification_by_Faith.jpg/255px-Luther_at_Erfurt_-_Justification_by_Faith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Luther_at_Erfurt_-_Justification_by_Faith.jpg/340px-Luther_at_Erfurt_-_Justification_by_Faith.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luther_at_Erfurt_-_Justification_by_Faith.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Luther at Erfurt", which depicts Martin Luther discovering the doctrine of <i>sola fide</i>. Painting by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Noel_Paton" title="Joseph Noel Paton">Joseph Noel Paton</a>, 1861.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From 1510 to 1520, Luther lectured on the Psalms, and on the books of Hebrews, Romans, and Galatians. As he studied these portions of the Bible, he came to view the use of terms such as <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">penance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Righteousness" title="Righteousness">righteousness</a> by the Catholic Church in new ways. He became convinced that the church was corrupt in its ways and had lost sight of what he saw as several of the central truths of Christianity. The most important for Luther was the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Justification_(theology)" title="Justification (theology)">justification</a> – God's act of declaring a sinner righteous – by faith alone through God's grace. He began to teach that salvation or redemption is a gift of God's <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">grace</a>, attainable only through faith in Jesus as the <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wriedt_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wriedt-42">[42]</a></sup> "This one and firm rock, which we call the doctrine of justification", he wrote, "is the chief article of the whole Christian doctrine, which comprehends the understanding of all godliness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther came to understand justification as entirely the work of God. This teaching by Luther was clearly expressed in his 1525 publication <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Bondage_of_the_Will" title="On the Bondage of the Will">On the Bondage of the Will</a></i>, which was written in response to <i>On Free Will</i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desiderius_Erasmus" title="Desiderius Erasmus">Desiderius Erasmus</a> (1524). Luther based his position on <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestination</a> on St. Paul's epistle to the <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Ephesians%202:8–10:8&amp;version=nkjv" rel="nofollow">Ephesians 2:8–10</a>. Against the teaching of his day that the righteous acts of believers are performed in <i>cooperation</i> with God, Luther wrote that Christians receive such righteousness entirely from outside themselves; that righteousness not only comes from Christ but actually <i>is</i> the righteousness of Christ, imputed to Christians (rather than infused into them) through faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>"That is why faith alone makes someone just and fulfills the law," he wrote. "Faith is that which brings the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a> through the merits of Christ."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faith_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-faith-45">[45]</a></sup> Faith, for Luther, was a gift from God; the experience of being justified by faith was "as though I had been born again." His entry into Paradise, no less, was a discovery about "the righteousness of God" – a discovery that "the just person" of whom the Bible speaks (as in Romans 1:17) lives by faith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> He explained his concept of "justification" in the <a href="/wiki/Smalcald_Articles" title="Smalcald Articles">Smalcald Articles</a>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The first and chief article is this: Jesus Christ, our God and Lord, died for our sins and was raised again for our justification (Romans 3:24–25). He alone is the Lamb of God who takes away the sins of the world (<a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">John</a> 1:29), and God has laid on Him the iniquity of us all (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Isaiah" title="Book of Isaiah">Isaiah</a> 53:6). All have sinned and are justified freely, without their own works and merits, by His grace, through the redemption that is in Christ Jesus, in His blood (Romans 3:23–25). This is necessary to believe. This cannot be otherwise acquired or grasped by any work, law or merit. Therefore, it is clear and certain that this faith alone justifies us ... Nothing of this article can be yielded or surrendered, even though heaven and earth and everything else falls (<a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">Mark</a> 13:31).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Luther's rediscovery of "Christ and His salvation" was the first of two points that became the foundation for the Reformation. His railing against the sale of indulgences was based on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFroom1948243_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFroom1948243-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breach_with_the_papacy">Breach with the papacy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bulla-contra-errores.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="587" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Bulla-contra-errores.jpg/220px-Bulla-contra-errores.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Bulla-contra-errores.jpg/330px-Bulla-contra-errores.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Bulla-contra-errores.jpg/440px-Bulla-contra-errores.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bulla-contra-errores.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_X" title="Pope Leo X">Pope Leo X</a>'s <i>Bull against the errors of Martin Luther</i>, 1521, commonly known as <i><a href="/wiki/Exsurge_Domine" title="Exsurge Domine">Exsurge Domine</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Albert_of_Brandenburg" title="Albert of Brandenburg">Archbishop Albrecht of Mainz and Magdeburg</a> did not reply to Luther's letter containing the <i>Ninety-five Theses</i>. He had the theses checked for heresy and in December 1517 forwarded them to Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> He needed the revenue from the indulgences to pay off a papal dispensation for his tenure of more than one bishopric. As Luther later noted, "the pope had a finger in the pie as well, because one half was to go to the building of St Peter's Church in Rome".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pope Leo X was used to reformers and heretics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> and he responded slowly, "with great care as is proper."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Over the next three years he deployed a series of papal theologians and envoys against Luther, which served only to harden the reformer's anti-papal theology. First, the Dominican theologian <a href="/wiki/Sylvester_Mazzolini" title="Sylvester Mazzolini">Sylvester Mazzolini</a> drafted a heresy case against Luther, whom Leo then summoned to Rome. The <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_Elector_of_Saxony" title="Frederick III, Elector of Saxony">Elector Frederick</a> persuaded the pope to have Luther examined at Augsburg, where the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Diet_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Imperial Diet (Holy Roman Empire)">Imperial Diet</a> was held.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>There, over a three-day period in October 1518, Luther defended himself under questioning by <a href="/wiki/Papal_legate" title="Papal legate">papal legate</a> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cajetan" title="Thomas Cajetan">Cardinal Cajetan</a>. The Pope's right to issue indulgences was at the centre of the dispute between the two men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reformation500_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reformation500-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ex-Classics_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ex-Classics-55">[55]</a></sup> The hearings degenerated into a shouting match. More than writing his theses, Luther's confrontation with the church cast him as an enemy of the pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Cajetan's original instructions had been to arrest Luther if he failed to recant, but the legate desisted from doing so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Luther slipped out of the city at night, unbeknownst to Cajetan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luther-vor-Cajetan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="800" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Luther-vor-Cajetan.jpg/220px-Luther-vor-Cajetan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Luther-vor-Cajetan.jpg/330px-Luther-vor-Cajetan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Luther-vor-Cajetan.jpg/440px-Luther-vor-Cajetan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luther-vor-Cajetan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The meeting of Martin Luther (right) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal_Cajetan" title="Cardinal Cajetan">Cardinal Cajetan</a> (left, holding the book).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In January 1519, at <a href="/wiki/Altenburg" title="Altenburg">Altenburg</a> in Saxony, the papal <i>nuncio</i> <a href="/wiki/Karl_von_Miltitz" title="Karl von Miltitz">Karl von Miltitz</a> adopted a more conciliatory approach. Luther made certain concessions to the Saxon, who was a relative of the Elector, and promised to remain silent if his opponents did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The theologian <a href="/wiki/Johann_Eck" title="Johann Eck">Johann Eck</a>, however, was determined to expose Luther's doctrine in a public forum. In June and July 1519, he staged a <a href="/wiki/Leipzig_Debate" title="Leipzig Debate">disputation</a> with Luther's colleague <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Karlstadt" title="Andreas Karlstadt">Andreas Karlstadt</a> at <a href="/wiki/Leipzig" title="Leipzig">Leipzig</a> and invited Luther to speak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther's boldest assertion in the debate was that <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Matthew</a> 16:18 does not confer on popes the exclusive right to interpret scripture, and that therefore neither popes nor <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_council" title="Ecumenical council">church councils</a> were infallible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> For this, Eck branded Luther a new <a href="/wiki/Jan_Hus" title="Jan Hus">Jan Hus</a>, referring to the Czech reformer and heretic <a href="/wiki/Death_by_burning" title="Death by burning">burned at the stake</a> in 1415. From that moment, he devoted himself to Luther's defeat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Excommunication">Excommunication</span></h3>
<p>On 15 June 1520, the Pope warned Luther with the <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">papal bull</a> (edict) <i><a href="/wiki/Exsurge_Domine" title="Exsurge Domine">Exsurge Domine</a></i> that he risked <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a> unless he recanted 41 sentences drawn from his writings, including the <i>Ninety-five Theses</i>, within 60 days. That autumn, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Eck" title="Johann Eck">Johann Eck</a> proclaimed the bull in Meissen and other towns. <a href="/wiki/Karl_von_Miltitz" title="Karl von Miltitz">Karl von Miltitz</a>, a papal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuncio" title="Nuncio">nuncio</a>, attempted to broker a solution, but Luther, who had sent the Pope a copy of <i>On the Freedom of a Christian</i> in October, publicly set fire to the bull and <a href="/wiki/Decretal" title="Decretal">decretals</a> at Wittenberg on 10 December 1520,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillerbrand463_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hillerbrand463-63">[63]</a></sup> an act he defended in <i>Why the Pope and his Recent Book are Burned</i> and <i>Assertions Concerning All Articles</i>. As a consequence, Luther was excommunicated by Pope Leo X on 3 January 1521, in the bull <i><a href="/wiki/Decet_Romanum_Pontificem" title="Decet Romanum Pontificem">Decet Romanum Pontificem</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet_of_Worms">Diet of Worms</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Worms" title="Diet of Worms">Diet of Worms</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="528" data-file-width="1000" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg/290px-Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg/435px-Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg/580px-Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luther_at_the_Diet_of_Worms.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Luther Before the Diet of Worms</i> by <a href="/wiki/Anton_von_Werner" title="Anton von Werner">Anton von Werner</a> (1843–1915).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The enforcement of the ban on the <i>Ninety-five Theses</i> fell to the secular authorities. On 18 April 1521, Luther appeared as ordered before the <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Worms" title="Diet of Worms">Diet of Worms</a>. This was a general assembly of the estates of the Holy Roman Empire that took place in <a href="/wiki/Worms,_Germany" title="Worms, Germany">Worms</a>, a town on the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a>. It was conducted from 28 January to 25 May 1521, with <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Charles V</a> presiding. Prince <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_Elector_of_Saxony" title="Frederick III, Elector of Saxony">Frederick III, Elector of Saxony</a>, obtained a <a href="/wiki/Safe_conduct" title="Safe conduct">safe conduct</a> for Luther to and from the meeting.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johann_Eck" title="Johann Eck">Johann Eck</a>, speaking on behalf of the Empire as assistant of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archbishopric_of_Trier" title="Archbishopric of Trier">Archbishop of Trier</a>, presented Luther with copies of his writings laid out on a table and asked him if the books were his, and whether he stood by their contents. Luther confirmed he was their author, but requested time to think about the answer to the second question. He prayed, consulted friends, and gave his response the next day:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Unless I am convinced by the testimony of the Scriptures or by clear reason (for I do not trust either in the pope or in councils alone, since it is well known that they have often erred and contradicted themselves), I am bound by the Scriptures I have quoted and my conscience is captive to the Word of God. I cannot and will not recant anything, since it is neither safe nor right to go against conscience. May God help me. Amen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>At the end of this speech, Luther raised his arm "in the traditional salute of a knight winning a bout." Michael Mullett considers this speech as a "world classic of epoch-making oratory."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mullettp25_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-mullettp25-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martin-Luther-Denkmal,_Worms.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Martin-Luther-Denkmal%2C_Worms.JPG/220px-Martin-Luther-Denkmal%2C_Worms.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Martin-Luther-Denkmal%2C_Worms.JPG/330px-Martin-Luther-Denkmal%2C_Worms.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Martin-Luther-Denkmal%2C_Worms.JPG/440px-Martin-Luther-Denkmal%2C_Worms.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martin-Luther-Denkmal,_Worms.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Martin Luther Memorial in Worms. His statue is surrounded by the figures of his lay protectors and earlier Church reformers including John Wycliffe, Jan Hus and Girolamo Savonarola.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eck informed Luther that he was acting like a heretic:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"'Martin,' said he, 'there is no one of the heresies which have torn the bosom of the church, which has not derived its origin from the various interpretation of the Scripture. The Bible itself is the arsenal whence each innovator has drawn his deceptive arguments. It was with Biblical texts that <a href="/wiki/Pelagius" title="Pelagius">Pelagius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arius" title="Arius">Arius</a> maintained their doctrines. Arius, for instance, found the negation of the eternity of the Word—an eternity which you admit, in this verse of the New Testament—<i>Joseph knew not his wife till she had brought forth her first-born son</i>; and he said, in the same way that you say, that this passage enchained him. When the fathers of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Constance" title="Council of Constance">Council of Constance</a> condemned this proposition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Huss" title="John Huss">John Huss</a>—<i>The church of Jesus Christ is only the community of the elect</i>, they condemned an error; for the church, like a good mother, embraces within her arms all who bear the name of Christian, all who are called to enjoy the celestial beatitude.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Luther refused to recant his writings. He is sometimes also quoted as saying: "Here I stand. I can do no other". Recent scholars consider the evidence for these words to be unreliable, since they were inserted before "May God help me" only in later versions of the speech and not recorded in witness accounts of the proceedings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> However, Mullett suggests that given his nature, "we are free to believe that Luther would tend to select the more dramatic form of words."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mullettp25_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-mullettp25-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Over the next five days, private conferences were held to determine Luther's fate. The Emperor presented the final draft of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edict_of_Worms" title="Edict of Worms">Edict of Worms</a> on 25 May 1521, declaring Luther an <a href="/wiki/Outlaw" title="Outlaw">outlaw</a>, banning his literature, and requiring his arrest: "We want him to be apprehended and punished as a notorious heretic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It also made it a crime for anyone in Germany to give Luther food or shelter. It permitted anyone to kill Luther without legal consequence.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="At_Wartburg_Castle">At Wartburg Castle</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wartburg_Eisenach_DSCN3512.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Wartburg_Eisenach_DSCN3512.jpg/220px-Wartburg_Eisenach_DSCN3512.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Wartburg_Eisenach_DSCN3512.jpg/330px-Wartburg_Eisenach_DSCN3512.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Wartburg_Eisenach_DSCN3512.jpg/440px-Wartburg_Eisenach_DSCN3512.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wartburg_Eisenach_DSCN3512.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Wartburg" title="Wartburg">Wartburg Castle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eisenach" title="Eisenach">Eisenach</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WartburgLutherstube1900.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2565" data-file-width="3496" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/WartburgLutherstube1900.jpg/220px-WartburgLutherstube1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/WartburgLutherstube1900.jpg/330px-WartburgLutherstube1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/WartburgLutherstube1900.jpg/440px-WartburgLutherstube1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WartburgLutherstube1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Wartburg" title="Wartburg">Wartburg</a> room where Luther translated the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> into German. An original first edition is kept in the case on the desk</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Luther's disappearance during his return to Wittenberg was planned. Frederick III had him intercepted on his way home in the forest near Wittenberg by masked horsemen impersonating highway robbers. They escorted Luther to the security of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wartburg_Castle" title="Wartburg Castle">Wartburg Castle</a> at Eisenach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> During his stay at Wartburg, which he referred to as "my <a href="/wiki/Patmos" title="Patmos">Patmos</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Luther translated the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> from Greek into German and poured out doctrinal and polemical writings. These included a renewed attack on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_of_Mainz" title="Albert of Mainz">Archbishop Albrecht of Mainz</a>, whom he shamed into halting the sale of indulgences in his episcopates,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and a "Refutation of the Argument of Latomus," in which he expounded the principle of <a href="/wiki/Justification_(theology)" title="Justification (theology)">justification</a> to <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Latomus" title="Jacobus Latomus">Jacobus Latomus</a>, an orthodox theologian from <a href="/wiki/Leuven" title="Leuven">Louvain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>In this work, one of his most emphatic statements on faith, he argued that every good work designed to attract God's favor is a sin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> All humans are sinners by nature, he explained, and <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">God's grace</a> (which cannot be earned) alone can make them just. On 1 August 1521, Luther wrote to Melanchthon on the same theme: "Be a sinner, and let your sins be strong, but let your trust in Christ be stronger, and rejoice in Christ who is the victor over sin, death, and the world. We will commit sins while we are here, for this life is not a place where justice resides."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sinsbestrong_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-sinsbestrong-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the summer of 1521, Luther widened his target from individual pieties like indulgences and pilgrimages to doctrines at the heart of Church practice. In <i>On the Abrogation of the Private Mass</i>, he condemned as idolatry the idea that the mass is a sacrifice, asserting instead that it is a gift, to be received with thanksgiving by the whole congregation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> His essay <i>On Confession, Whether the Pope has the Power to Require It</i> rejected compulsory <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">confession</a> and encouraged private confession and <a href="/wiki/Absolution" title="Absolution">absolution</a>, since "every Christian is a confessor."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> In November, Luther wrote <i>The Judgement of Martin Luther on Monastic Vows</i>. He assured monks and nuns that they could break their vows without sin, because vows were an illegitimate and vain attempt to win salvation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_Luthers_als_Junker_J%C3%B6rg_(Leipzig).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2687" data-file-width="2020" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_Luthers_als_Junker_J%C3%B6rg_%28Leipzig%29.jpg/170px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_Luthers_als_Junker_J%C3%B6rg_%28Leipzig%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_Luthers_als_Junker_J%C3%B6rg_%28Leipzig%29.jpg/255px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_Luthers_als_Junker_J%C3%B6rg_%28Leipzig%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_Luthers_als_Junker_J%C3%B6rg_%28Leipzig%29.jpg/340px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_Luthers_als_Junker_J%C3%B6rg_%28Leipzig%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_Luthers_als_Junker_J%C3%B6rg_(Leipzig).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Luther disguised as "<a href="/wiki/Junker" title="Junker">Junker</a> Jörg", 1521</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1521 Luther dealt largely with prophecy, in which he broadened the foundations of the Reformation, placing them on prophetic faith. His main interest was centered on the prophecy of the Little Horn in Daniel 8:9–12, 23–25. The antichrist of 2 Thessalonians 2 was identified as the power of the Papacy. So too was the Little Horn of Daniel 7, coming up among the divisions of Rome, explicitly applied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFroom1948261_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFroom1948261-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther made his pronouncements from Wartburg in the context of rapid developments at Wittenberg, of which he was kept fully informed. <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Karlstadt" title="Andreas Karlstadt">Andreas Karlstadt</a>, supported by the ex-Augustinian <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Zwilling" title="Gabriel Zwilling">Gabriel Zwilling</a>, embarked on a radical programme of reform there in June 1521, exceeding anything envisaged by Luther. The reforms provoked disturbances, including a revolt by the Augustinian friars against their prior, the smashing of statues and images in churches, and denunciations of the magistracy. After secretly visiting Wittenberg in early December 1521, Luther wrote <i>A Sincere Admonition by Martin Luther to All Christians to Guard Against Insurrection and Rebellion</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Wittenberg became even more volatile after Christmas when a band of visionary zealots, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Zwickau_prophets" title="Zwickau prophets">Zwickau prophets</a>, arrived, preaching revolutionary doctrines such as the equality of man, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">adult baptism</a>, and Christ's imminent return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> When the town council asked Luther to return, he decided it was his duty to act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Return_to_Wittenberg_and_Peasants'_War"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_to_Wittenberg_and_Peasants.27_War">Return to Wittenberg and Peasants' War</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Radical_Reformation" title="Radical Reformation">Radical Reformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_Peasants%27_War" title="German Peasants' War">German Peasants' War</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wittenberg_Lutherhaus.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Wittenberg_Lutherhaus.JPG/220px-Wittenberg_Lutherhaus.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Wittenberg_Lutherhaus.JPG/330px-Wittenberg_Lutherhaus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Wittenberg_Lutherhaus.JPG/440px-Wittenberg_Lutherhaus.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wittenberg_Lutherhaus.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lutherhaus" title="Lutherhaus">Lutherhaus</a>, Luther's residence in <a href="/wiki/Wittenberg" title="Wittenberg">Wittenberg</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Luther secretly returned to Wittenberg on 6 March 1522. He wrote to the Elector: "During my absence, Satan has entered my sheepfold, and committed ravages which I cannot repair by writing, but only by my personal presence and living word."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> For eight days in <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a>, beginning on <i>Invocavit</i> Sunday, 9 March, Luther preached eight sermons, which became known as the "Invocavit Sermons". In these sermons, he hammered home the primacy of core <a href="/wiki/Christian_values" title="Christian values">Christian values</a> such as love, patience, charity, and freedom, and reminded the citizens to trust God's word rather than violence to bring about necessary change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>Do you know what the Devil thinks when he sees men use violence to propagate the gospel? He sits with folded arms behind the fire of hell, and says with malignant looks and frightful grin: "Ah, how wise these madmen are to play my game! Let them go on; I shall reap the benefit. I delight in it." But when he sees the Word running and contending alone on the battle-field, then he shudders and shakes for fear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaff_IV_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaff_IV-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The effect of Luther's intervention was immediate. After the sixth sermon, the Wittenberg jurist Jerome Schurf wrote to the elector: "Oh, what joy has Dr. Martin's return spread among us! His words, through divine mercy, are bringing back every day misguided people into the way of the truth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaff_IV_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schaff_IV-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther next set about reversing or modifying the new church practices. By working alongside the authorities to restore public order, he signalled his reinvention as a conservative force within the Reformation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> After banishing the Zwickau prophets, he now faced a battle against not only the established Church but also the radical reformers who threatened the new order by fomenting social unrest and violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titelblatt_12_Artikel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="723" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Titelblatt_12_Artikel.jpg/220px-Titelblatt_12_Artikel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Titelblatt_12_Artikel.jpg/330px-Titelblatt_12_Artikel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Titelblatt_12_Artikel.jpg/440px-Titelblatt_12_Artikel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titelblatt_12_Artikel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i><a href="/wiki/Twelve_Articles" title="Twelve Articles">Twelve Articles</a></i>, 1525.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite his victory in Wittenberg, Luther was unable to stifle radicalism further afield. Preachers such as Zwickau prophet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicholas_Storch" title="Nicholas Storch">Nicholas Storch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_M%C3%BCntzer" title="Thomas Müntzer">Thomas Müntzer</a> found support amongst poorer towns-people and peasants between 1521 and 1525. There had been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popular_revolt_in_late_medieval_Europe" title="Popular revolt in late medieval Europe">revolts</a> by the peasantry on a smaller scale since the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Luther's pamphlets against the Church and the hierarchy, often worded with "liberal" phraseology, now led many peasants to believe he would support an attack on the upper classes in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Revolts broke out in <a href="/wiki/Franconia" title="Franconia">Franconia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thuringia" title="Thuringia">Thuringia</a> in 1524, even drawing support from disaffected nobles, many of whom were in debt. Gaining momentum under the leadership of radicals such as Müntzer in Thuringia, and Hipler and Lotzer in the south-west, the revolts turned into war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther sympathised with some of the peasants' grievances, as he showed in his response to the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Articles" title="Twelve Articles">Twelve Articles</a> in May 1525, but he reminded the aggrieved to obey the temporal authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> During a tour of Thuringia, he became enraged at the widespread burning of convents, monasteries, bishops' palaces, and libraries. In <i><a href="/wiki/Against_the_Murderous,_Thieving_Hordes_of_Peasants" title="Against the Murderous, Thieving Hordes of Peasants">Against the Murderous, Thieving Hordes of Peasants</a></i>, written on his return to Wittenberg, he gave his interpretation of the Gospel teaching on wealth, condemned the violence as the devil's work, and called for the nobles to put down the rebels like mad dogs:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Therefore let everyone who can, smite, slay, and stab, secretly or openly, remembering that nothing can be more poisonous, hurtful, or devilish than a rebel ... For baptism does not make men free in body and property, but in soul; and the gospel does not make goods common, except in the case of those who, of their own <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a>, do what the apostles and disciples did in Acts 4 [:32–37]. They did not demand, as do our insane peasants in their raging, that the goods of others—of Pilate and Herod—should be common, but only their own goods. Our peasants, however, want to make the goods of other men common, and keep their own for themselves. Fine Christians they are! I think there is not a devil left in hell; they have all gone into the peasants. Their raving has gone beyond all measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Luther justified his opposition to the rebels on three grounds. First, in choosing violence over lawful submission to the secular government, they were ignoring Christ's counsel to "Render unto Caesar the things that are Caesar's"; St. Paul had written in his epistle to the <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Romans%2013:1–7:1&amp;version=nkjv" rel="nofollow">Romans 13:1–7</a> that all authorities are appointed by God and therefore should not be resisted. This reference from the Bible forms the foundation for the doctrine known as the <a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">divine right of kings</a>, or, in the German case, the divine right of the princes. Second, the violent actions of rebelling, robbing, and plundering placed the peasants "outside the law of God and Empire", so they deserved "death in body and soul, if only as highwaymen and murderers." Lastly, Luther charged the rebels with blasphemy for calling themselves "Christian brethren" and committing their sinful acts under the banner of the Gospel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Without Luther's backing for the uprising, many rebels laid down their weapons; others felt betrayed. Their defeat by the <a href="/wiki/Swabian_League" title="Swabian League">Swabian League</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Frankenhausen" title="Battle of Frankenhausen">Battle of Frankenhausen</a> on 15 May 1525, followed by Müntzer's execution, brought the revolutionary stage of the Reformation to a close.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Thereafter, radicalism found a refuge in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">Anabaptist</a> movement and other religious movements, while Luther's Reformation flourished under the wing of the secular powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In 1526 Luther wrote: “I, Martin Luther, have during the rebellion slain all the peasants, for it was I who ordered them to be struck dead.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage">Marriage</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_der_Katharina_von_Bora_(Christie%E2%80%99s_2001).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1928" data-file-width="1247" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_der_Katharina_von_Bora_%28Christie%E2%80%99s_2001%29.jpg/170px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_der_Katharina_von_Bora_%28Christie%E2%80%99s_2001%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_der_Katharina_von_Bora_%28Christie%E2%80%99s_2001%29.jpg/255px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_der_Katharina_von_Bora_%28Christie%E2%80%99s_2001%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_der_Katharina_von_Bora_%28Christie%E2%80%99s_2001%29.jpg/340px-Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_der_Katharina_von_Bora_%28Christie%E2%80%99s_2001%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lucas_Cranach_d.%C3%84._-_Bildnis_der_Katharina_von_Bora_(Christie%E2%80%99s_2001).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Katharina_von_Bora" title="Katharina von Bora">Katharina von Bora</a>, Luther's wife, by <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a>, 1526.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Martin Luther married <a href="/wiki/Katharina_von_Bora" title="Katharina von Bora">Katharina von Bora</a>, one of 12 nuns he had helped escape from the Nimbschen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cistercian_convent" title="Cistercian convent">Cistercian convent</a> in April 1523, when he arranged for them to be smuggled out in herring barrels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> "Suddenly, and while I was occupied with far different thoughts," he wrote to Wenceslaus Link, "the Lord has plunged me into marriage."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> At the time of their marriage, Katharina was 26 years old and Luther was 41 years old.</p>
<p>On 13 June 1525, the couple was engaged with <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Bugenhagen" title="Johannes Bugenhagen">Johannes Bugenhagen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justus_Jonas" title="Justus Jonas">Justus Jonas</a>, Johannes Apel, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a> and his wife as witnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheible147_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scheible147-99">[99]</a></sup> On the evening of the same day, the couple was married by Bugenhagen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheible147_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scheible147-99">[99]</a></sup> The ceremonial walk to the church and the wedding banquet were left out, and were made up two weeks later on 27 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheible147_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-Scheible147-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some priests and former members of <a href="/wiki/Catholic_religious_order" title="Catholic religious order">religious orders</a> had already married, including <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Karlstadt" title="Andreas Karlstadt">Andreas Karlstadt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Justus_Jonas" title="Justus Jonas">Justus Jonas</a>, but Luther's wedding set the seal of approval on clerical marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> He had long condemned vows of <a href="/wiki/Celibacy" title="Celibacy">celibacy</a> on Biblical grounds, but his decision to marry surprised many, not least Melanchthon, who called it reckless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Luther had written to <a href="/wiki/George_Spalatin" title="George Spalatin">George Spalatin</a> on 30 November 1524, "I shall never take a wife, as I feel at present. Not that I am insensible to my flesh or sex (for I am neither wood nor stone); but my mind is averse to wedlock because I daily expect the death of a heretic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Before marrying, Luther had been living on the plainest food, and, as he admitted himself, his mildewed bed was not properly made for months at a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther and his wife moved into a former monastery, "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augusteum_and_Lutherhaus" title="Augusteum and Lutherhaus">The Black Cloister</a>," a wedding present from the new elector <a href="/wiki/John,_Elector_of_Saxony" title="John, Elector of Saxony">John the Steadfast</a> (1525–32). They embarked on what appears to have been a happy and successful marriage, though money was often short.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Katharina bore six children: Hans – June 1526; <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_Luther" title="Elisabeth Luther">Elizabeth</a> – 10 December 1527, who died within a few months; <a href="/wiki/Magdalena_Luther" title="Magdalena Luther">Magdalene</a> – 1529, who died in Luther's arms in 1542; Martin – 1531; <a href="/wiki/Paul_Luther" title="Paul Luther">Paul</a> – January 1533; and Margaret – 1534; and she helped the couple earn a living by farming and taking in boarders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Luther confided to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Stiefel" title="Michael Stiefel">Michael Stiefel</a> on 11 August 1526: "My Katie is in all things so obliging and pleasing to me that I would not exchange my poverty for the riches of <a href="/wiki/Croesus" title="Croesus">Croesus</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organising_the_church">Organising the church</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kirchenordnung_Mecklenburg_1650.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="917" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Kirchenordnung_Mecklenburg_1650.jpg/220px-Kirchenordnung_Mecklenburg_1650.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Kirchenordnung_Mecklenburg_1650.jpg/330px-Kirchenordnung_Mecklenburg_1650.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Kirchenordnung_Mecklenburg_1650.jpg/440px-Kirchenordnung_Mecklenburg_1650.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kirchenordnung_Mecklenburg_1650.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Church orders, Mecklenburg 1650.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 1526, Luther found himself increasingly occupied in organising a new church. His Biblical ideal of congregations choosing their own ministers had proved unworkable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> According to Bainton: "Luther's dilemma was that he wanted both a confessional church based on personal faith and experience and a territorial church including all in a given locality. If he were forced to choose, he would take his stand with the masses, and this was the direction in which he moved."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1525 to 1529, he established a supervisory church body, laid down a new form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Service_of_worship" title="Service of worship">worship service</a>, and wrote a clear summary of the new faith in the form of two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catechisms" title="Catechisms">catechisms</a>. Luther's thought is revolutionary to the extent that it is a theology of the cross, the negation of every affirmation: as long as the cross is at the center, the system building tendency of reason is held in check, and system building does not degenerate into System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schroeder2000_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schroeder2000-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>To avoid confusing or upsetting the people, Luther avoided extreme change. He also did not wish to replace one controlling system with another. He concentrated on the church in the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">Electorate of Saxony</a>, acting only as an adviser to churches in new territories, many of which followed his Saxon model.
