He worked closely with the new elector, <a href="/wiki/John,_Elector_of_Saxony" title="John, Elector of Saxony">John the Steadfast</a>, to whom he turned for secular leadership and funds on behalf of a church largely shorn of its assets and income after the break with Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> For Luther's biographer Martin Brecht, this partnership "was the beginning of a questionable and originally unintended development towards a church government under the temporal sovereign".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>The elector authorised a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canonical_Visitation" title="Canonical Visitation">visitation</a> of the church, a power formerly exercised by bishops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> At times, Luther's practical reforms fell short of his earlier radical pronouncements. For example, the <i>Instructions for the Visitors of Parish Pastors in Electoral Saxony</i> (1528), drafted by Melanchthon with Luther's approval, stressed the role of repentance in the forgiveness of sins, despite Luther's position that faith alone ensures justification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eisleben" title="Eisleben">Eisleben</a> reformer <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Agricola" title="Johannes Agricola">Johannes Agricola</a> challenged this compromise, and Luther condemned him for teaching that faith is separate from works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The <i>Instruction</i> is a problematic document for those seeking a consistent evolution in Luther's thought and practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luckau_Nikolaikirche_Abendmahlsbild.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1817" data-file-width="2511" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Luckau_Nikolaikirche_Abendmahlsbild.jpg/220px-Luckau_Nikolaikirche_Abendmahlsbild.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Luckau_Nikolaikirche_Abendmahlsbild.jpg/330px-Luckau_Nikolaikirche_Abendmahlsbild.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Luckau_Nikolaikirche_Abendmahlsbild.jpg/440px-Luckau_Nikolaikirche_Abendmahlsbild.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luckau_Nikolaikirche_Abendmahlsbild.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lutheran church liturgy and sacraments.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In response to demands for a German <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a>, Luther wrote a <i><a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Messe" title="Deutsche Messe">German Mass</a></i>, which he published in early 1526.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> He did not intend it as a replacement for his 1523 adaptation of the Latin Mass but as an alternative for the "simple people", a "public stimulation for people to believe and become Christians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Luther based his order on the Catholic service but omitted "everything that smacks of sacrifice", and the Mass became a celebration where everyone received the wine as well as the bread.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> He retained the <a href="/wiki/Elevation_(liturgy)" title="Elevation (liturgy)">elevation of the host</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chalice" title="Chalice">chalice</a>, while trappings such as the Mass <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vestments" title="Vestments">vestments</a>, altar, and candles were made optional, allowing freedom of ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some reformers, including followers of <a href="/wiki/Huldrych_Zwingli" title="Huldrych Zwingli">Huldrych Zwingli</a>, considered Luther's service too papistic, and modern scholars note the conservatism of his alternative to the Catholic mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Luther's service, however, included congregational singing of hymns and psalms in German, as well as of parts of the liturgy, including Luther's unison setting of the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles' Creed">Creed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> To reach the simple people and the young, Luther incorporated religious instruction into the weekday services in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Catechism" title="Catechism">catechism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> He also provided simplified versions of the baptism and marriage services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther and his colleagues introduced the new order of worship during their visitation of the <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">Electorate of Saxony</a>, which began in 1527.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> They also assessed the standard of pastoral care and Christian education in the territory. "Merciful God, what misery I have seen," Luther wrote, "the common people knowing nothing at all of Christian doctrine ... and unfortunately many pastors are well-nigh unskilled and incapable of teaching."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catechisms">Catechisms</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MartinLutherWindow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4280" data-file-width="1841" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/MartinLutherWindow.jpg/130px-MartinLutherWindow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/MartinLutherWindow.jpg/195px-MartinLutherWindow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/MartinLutherWindow.jpg/260px-MartinLutherWindow.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MartinLutherWindow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A stained glass portrayal of Luther.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Luther devised the catechism as a method of imparting the basics of Christianity to the congregations. In 1529, he wrote the <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Large_Catechism" title="Luther's Large Catechism"><i>Large Catechism</i></a>, a manual for pastors and teachers, as well as a synopsis, the <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Small_Catechism" title="Luther's Small Catechism"><i>Small Catechism</i></a>, to be memorised by the people themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> The catechisms provided easy-to-understand instructional and devotional material on the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles' Creed">Apostles' Creed</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord's Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baptism#Protestant_Reformation" title="Baptism">baptism</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism#Lord.27s_Supper" title="Lutheranism">Lord's Supper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Luther incorporated questions and answers in the catechism so that the basics of Christian faith would not just be <a href="/wiki/Rote_learning" title="Rote learning">learned by rote</a>, "the way monkeys do it", but understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>The catechism is one of Luther's most personal works. "Regarding the plan to collect my writings in volumes," he wrote, "I am quite cool and not at all eager about it because, roused by a Saturnian hunger, I would rather see them all devoured. For I acknowledge none of them to be really a book of mine, except perhaps the <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Bondage_of_the_Will" title="On the Bondage of the Will">Bondage of the Will</a></i> and the Catechism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The <i>Small Catechism</i> has earned a reputation as a model of clear religious teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> It remains in use today, along with Luther's hymns and his translation of the Bible.</p>
<p>Luther's <i>Small Catechism</i> proved especially effective in helping parents teach their children; likewise the <i>Larger Catechism</i> was effective for pastors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Using the German vernacular, they expressed the Apostles' Creed in simpler, more personal, <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinitarian</a> language. He rewrote each article of the Creed to express the character of the Father, the Son, or the Holy Spirit. Luther's goal was to enable the catechumens to see themselves as a personal object of the work of the three persons of the Trinity, each of which works in the catechumen's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_Arne_Nestingen_1996_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-James_Arne_Nestingen_1996-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>That is, Luther depicted the Trinity not as a doctrine to be learned, but as persons to be known. The Father creates, the Son redeems, and the Spirit sanctifies, a divine unity with separate personalities. Salvation originates with the Father and draws the believer to the Father. Luther's treatment of the Apostles' Creed must be understood in the context of the Decalogue (the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord's Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a>, which are also part of the Lutheran catechetical teaching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-James_Arne_Nestingen_1996_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-James_Arne_Nestingen_1996-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Translation_of_the_Bible">Translation of the Bible</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Luther_Bible" title="Luther Bible">Luther Bible</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="759" data-file-width="1000" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/220px-Lutherbibel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/330px-Lutherbibel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/440px-Lutherbibel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Luther's 1534 Bible.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Luther had published his German translation of the New Testament in 1522, and he and his collaborators completed the translation of the Old Testament in 1534, when the whole Bible was published. He continued to work on refining the translation until the end of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Others had translated the Bible into German, but Luther tailored his translation to his own doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> When he was criticised for inserting the word "alone" after "faith" in <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Romans" title="Epistle to the Romans">Romans</a> 3:28,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> he replied in part: "[T]he text itself and the meaning of St. Paul urgently require and demand it. For in that very passage he is dealing with the main point of Christian doctrine, namely, that we are justified by faith in Christ without any works of the Law. ... But when works are so completely cut away – and that must mean that faith alone justifies – whoever would speak plainly and clearly about this cutting away of works will have to say, 'Faith alone justifies us, and not works'."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther's translation used the variant of German spoken at the Saxon chancellery, intelligible to both northern and southern Germans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> He intended his vigorous, direct language to make the Bible accessible to everyday Germans, "for we are removing impediments and difficulties so that other people may read it without hindrance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>Published at a time of rising demand for German-language publications, Luther's version quickly became a popular and influential Bible translation. As such, it made a significant contribution to the evolution of the German language and literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Furnished with notes and prefaces by Luther, and with woodcuts by <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach</a> that contained anti-papal imagery, it played a major role in the spread of Luther's doctrine throughout Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> The Luther Bible influenced other vernacular translations, such as <a href="/wiki/William_Tyndale" title="William Tyndale">William Tyndale</a>'s English Bible (1525 forward), a precursor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authorised_King_James_Version" title="Authorised King James Version">King James Bible</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hymnodist">Hymnodist</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_hymns_by_Martin_Luther" title="List of hymns by Martin Luther">List of hymns by Martin Luther</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EinFesteBurg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2511" data-file-width="2820" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/90/EinFesteBurg.jpg/260px-EinFesteBurg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/90/EinFesteBurg.jpg/390px-EinFesteBurg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/90/EinFesteBurg.jpg/520px-EinFesteBurg.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EinFesteBurg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An early printing of Luther's hymn "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ein_feste_Burg_ist_unser_Gott" title="Ein feste Burg ist unser Gott">Ein feste Burg ist unser Gott</a>"</div>
</div>
</div>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">

<td class="mbox-image">

<div>

<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
<hr/>
<span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Luther was a prolific <a href="/wiki/Hymnodist" title="Hymnodist">hymnodist</a>, authoring hymns such as "Ein feste Burg ist unser Gott" ("<a href="/wiki/A_Mighty_Fortress_Is_Our_God" title="A Mighty Fortress Is Our God">A Mighty Fortress Is Our God</a>"), based on <a href="/wiki/Psalm_46" title="Psalm 46">Psalm 46</a>, and "<a href="/wiki/Vom_Himmel_hoch,_da_komm_ich_her" title="Vom Himmel hoch, da komm ich her">Vom Himmel hoch, da komm ich her</a>" ("From Heaven Above to Earth I Come"), based on Luke 2:11–12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Luther connected high art and folk music, also all classes, clergy and laity, men, women and children. His tool of choice for this connection was the singing of German hymns in connection with worship, school, home, and the public arena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christopher_Boyd_Brown_2005_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christopher_Boyd_Brown_2005-143">[143]</a></sup> He often accompanied the sung hymns with a lute, later recreated as the <a href="/wiki/Waldzither" title="Waldzither">waldzither</a> that became a <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_instruments_(music)" title="List of national instruments (music)">national instrument</a> of Germany in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther's hymns were frequently evoked by particular events in his life and the unfolding Reformation. This behavior started with his learning of the execution of <a href="/wiki/Johann_Esch_and_Heinrich_Voes" title="Johann Esch and Heinrich Voes">Johann Esch and Heinrich Voes</a>, the first individuals to be martyred by the Roman Catholic Church for Lutheran views, prompting Luther to write the hymn "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ein_neues_Lied_wir_heben_an" title="Ein neues Lied wir heben an">Ein neues Lied wir heben an</a>" ("A new song we raise"), which is generally known in English by John C. Messenger's translation by the title and first line "Flung to the Heedless Winds" and sung to the tune <a href="/wiki/Ibstone" title="Ibstone">Ibstone</a> composed in 1875 by Maria C. Tiddeman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther's 1524 creedal hymn "<span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Wir_glauben_all_an_einen_Gott" title="Wir glauben all an einen Gott">Wir glauben all an einen Gott</a></span>" ("We All Believe in One True God") is a three-stanza confession of faith prefiguring Luther's 1529 three-part explanation of the Apostles' Creed in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Small_Catechism" title="Small Catechism">Small Catechism</a>.</i> Luther's hymn, adapted and expanded from an earlier German creedal hymn, gained widespread use in vernacular Lutheran liturgies as early as 1525. Sixteenth-century Lutheran hymnals also included "Wir glauben all" among the catechetical hymns, although 18th-century hymnals tended to label the hymn as <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinitarian</a> rather than catechetical, and 20th-century Lutherans rarely used the hymn because of the perceived difficulty of its tune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christopher_Boyd_Brown_2005_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Christopher_Boyd_Brown_2005-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luther_Vaterunser_001.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1158" data-file-width="404" height="430" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Luther_Vaterunser_001.jpg/150px-Luther_Vaterunser_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Luther_Vaterunser_001.jpg/225px-Luther_Vaterunser_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Luther_Vaterunser_001.jpg/300px-Luther_Vaterunser_001.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luther_Vaterunser_002.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3488" data-file-width="1242" height="421" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Luther_Vaterunser_002.jpg/150px-Luther_Vaterunser_002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Luther_Vaterunser_002.jpg/225px-Luther_Vaterunser_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Luther_Vaterunser_002.jpg/300px-Luther_Vaterunser_002.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Autograph of "<a href="/wiki/Vater_unser_im_Himmelreich" title="Vater unser im Himmelreich">Vater unser im Himmelreich</a>", with the only notes extant in Luther's handwriting</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Luther's 1538 hymnic version of the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Prayer" title="Lord's Prayer">Lord's Prayer</a>, "<a href="/wiki/Vater_unser_im_Himmelreich" title="Vater unser im Himmelreich">Vater unser im Himmelreich</a>", corresponds exactly to Luther's explanation of the prayer in the <i>Small Catechism</i>, with one stanza for each of the seven prayer petitions, plus opening and closing stanzas. The hymn functioned both as a liturgical setting of the Lord's Prayer and as a means of examining candidates on specific catechism questions. The extant manuscript shows multiple revisions, demonstrating Luther's concern to clarify and strengthen the text and to provide an appropriately prayerful tune. Other 16th- and 20th-century versifications of the Lord's Prayer have adopted Luther's tune, although modern texts are considerably shorter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther wrote "<a href="/wiki/Aus_tiefer_Not_schrei_ich_zu_dir" title="Aus tiefer Not schrei ich zu dir">Aus tiefer Not schrei ich zu dir</a>" ("From depths of woe I cry to You") in 1523 as a hymnic version of <a href="/wiki/Psalm_130" title="Psalm 130">Psalm 130</a> and sent it as a sample to encourage his colleagues to write psalm-hymns for use in German worship. In a collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Paul_Speratus" title="Paul Speratus">Paul Speratus</a>, this and seven other hymns were published in the <i>Achtliederbuch</i>, the <a href="/wiki/First_Lutheran_hymnal" title="First Lutheran hymnal">first Lutheran hymnal</a>. In 1524 Luther developed his original four-stanza psalm paraphrase into a five-stanza Reformation hymn that developed the theme of "grace alone" more fully. Because it expressed essential Reformation doctrine, this expanded version of "Aus tiefer Not" was designated as a regular component of several regional Lutheran liturgies and was widely used at funerals, including Luther's own. Along with Erhart Hegenwalt's hymnic version of <a href="/wiki/Psalm_51" title="Psalm 51">Psalm 51</a>, Luther's expanded hymn was also adopted for use with the fifth part of Luther's catechism, concerning confession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther wrote "<a href="/wiki/Ach_Gott,_vom_Himmel_sieh_darein" title="Ach Gott, vom Himmel sieh darein">Ach Gott, vom Himmel sieh darein</a>" ("Oh God, look down from heaven"). "<a href="/wiki/Nun_komm,_der_Heiden_Heiland" title="Nun komm, der Heiden Heiland">Nun komm, der Heiden Heiland</a>" (Now come, Savior of the gentiles), based on <i><a href="/wiki/Veni_redemptor_gentium" title="Veni redemptor gentium">Veni redemptor gentium</a></i>, became the main hymn (Hauptlied) for <a href="/wiki/Advent" title="Advent">Advent</a>. He transformed <i>A solus ortus cardine</i> to "<span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Christum_wir_sollen_loben_schon" title="Christum wir sollen loben schon">Christum wir sollen loben schon</a></span>" ("We should now praise Christ") and <i><a href="/wiki/Veni_Creator_Spiritus" title="Veni Creator Spiritus">Veni Creator Spiritus</a></i> to "<span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Komm,_Gott_Sch%C3%B6pfer,_Heiliger_Geist" title="Komm, Gott Schöpfer, Heiliger Geist">Komm, Gott Schöpfer, Heiliger Geist</a></span>" ("Come, Holy Spirit, Lord God").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>He wrote two hymns on the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a>, "<span lang="de"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dies_sind_die_heilgen_Zehn_Gebot" title="Dies sind die heilgen Zehn Gebot">Dies sind die heilgen Zehn Gebot</a></span>" and "Mensch, willst du leben seliglich". His "<a href="/wiki/Gelobet_seist_du,_Jesu_Christ" title="Gelobet seist du, Jesu Christ">Gelobet seist du, Jesu Christ</a>" ("Praise be to You, Jesus Christ") became the main hymn for Christmas. He wrote for <a href="/wiki/Pentecost" title="Pentecost">Pentecost</a> "<a href="/wiki/Nun_bitten_wir_den_Heiligen_Geist" title="Nun bitten wir den Heiligen Geist">Nun bitten wir den Heiligen Geist</a>", and adopted for Easter "<span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Christ_ist_erstanden" title="Christ ist erstanden">Christ ist erstanden</a></span>" (Christ is risen), based on <i><a href="/wiki/Victimae_paschali_laudes" title="Victimae paschali laudes">Victimae paschali laudes</a></i>. "<span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Mit_Fried_und_Freud_ich_fahr_dahin" title="Mit Fried und Freud ich fahr dahin">Mit Fried und Freud ich fahr dahin</a></span>", a paraphrase of <a href="/wiki/Nunc_dimittis" title="Nunc dimittis">Nunc dimittis</a>, was intended for <a href="/wiki/Presentation_of_Jesus_at_the_Temple" title="Presentation of Jesus at the Temple">Purification</a>, but became also a funeral hymn. He paraphrased the <a href="/wiki/Te_Deum" title="Te Deum">Te Deum</a> as "<a href="/wiki/Herr_Gott,_dich_loben_wir" title="Herr Gott, dich loben wir">Herr Gott, dich loben wir</a>" with a simplified form of the melody. It became known as the German Te Deum.</p>
<p>Luther's 1541 hymn "<span lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Christ_unser_Herr_zum_Jordan_kam" title="Christ unser Herr zum Jordan kam">Christ unser Herr zum Jordan kam</a></span>" ("To Jordan came the Christ our Lord") reflects the structure and substance of his questions and answers concerning baptism in the <i>Small Catechism</i>. Luther adopted a preexisting <a href="/wiki/Johann_Walter" title="Johann Walter">Johann Walter</a> tune associated with a hymnic setting of <a href="/wiki/Psalm_67" title="Psalm 67">Psalm 67</a>'s prayer for grace; Wolf Heintz's four-part setting of the hymn was used to introduce the Lutheran Reformation in Halle in 1541. Preachers and composers of the 18th century, including <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">J. S. Bach</a>, used this rich hymn as a subject for their own work, although its objective baptismal theology was displaced by more subjective hymns under the influence of late-19th-century Lutheran <a href="/wiki/Pietism" title="Pietism">pietism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christopher_Boyd_Brown_2005_143-2"><a href="#cite_note-Christopher_Boyd_Brown_2005-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther's hymns were included in early Lutheran hymnals and spread the ideas of the Reformation. He supplied four of eight songs of the <a href="/wiki/First_Lutheran_hymnal" title="First Lutheran hymnal">First Lutheran hymnal</a> <i>Achtliederbuch</i>, 18 of 26 songs of the <i><a href="/wiki/Erfurt_Enchiridion" title="Erfurt Enchiridion">Erfurt Enchiridion</a></i>, and 24 of the 32 songs in the first choral hymnal with settings by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Walter" title="Johann Walter">Johann Walter</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Eyn_geystlich_Gesangk_Buchleyn" title="Eyn geystlich Gesangk Buchleyn">Eyn geystlich Gesangk Buchleyn</a></i>, all published in 1524.</p>
<p>Luther's hymns inspired composers to write music. <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Johann Sebastian Bach</a> included several verses as <a href="/wiki/Chorale" title="Chorale">chorales</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Bach_cantata" title="Bach cantata">cantatas</a> and based <a href="/wiki/Chorale_cantata_(Bach)" title="Chorale cantata (Bach)">chorale cantatas</a> entirely on them, namely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_4" title="BWV 4"><i>Christ lag in Todes Banden</i>, BWV 4</a>, as early as possibly 1707, in his second annual cycle (1724 to 1725) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_2" title="BWV 2"><i>Ach Gott, vom Himmel sieh darein</i>, BWV 2</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_7" title="BWV 7"><i>Christ unser Herr zum Jordan kam</i>, BWV 7</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_62" title="BWV 62"><i>Nun komm, der Heiden Heiland</i>, BWV 62</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_91" title="BWV 91"><i>Gelobet seist du, Jesu Christ</i>, BWV 91</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_38" title="BWV 38"><i>Aus tiefer Not schrei ich zu dir</i>, BWV 38</a>, later <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_80" title="BWV 80"><i>Ein feste Burg ist unser Gott</i>, BWV 80</a>, and in 1735 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BWV_14" title="BWV 14"><i>Wär Gott nicht mit uns diese Zeit</i>, BWV 14</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_soul_after_death">On the soul after death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Epi_Michael-Meienburg-1555.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="917" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Epi_Michael-Meienburg-1555.jpg/220px-Epi_Michael-Meienburg-1555.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Epi_Michael-Meienburg-1555.jpg/330px-Epi_Michael-Meienburg-1555.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Epi_Michael-Meienburg-1555.jpg/440px-Epi_Michael-Meienburg-1555.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Epi_Michael-Meienburg-1555.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Luther on the left with <a href="/wiki/Lazarus_of_Bethany" title="Lazarus of Bethany">Lazarus</a> being raised by Jesus from the dead, painting by <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a>, 1558.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In contrast to the views of <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> throughout his life Luther maintained that it was not false doctrine to believe that a Christian's soul sleeps after it is separated from the body in death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Accordingly, he disputed traditional interpretations of some Bible passages, such as the parable of the <a href="/wiki/Rich_man_and_Lazarus" title="Rich man and Lazarus">rich man and Lazarus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> This also led Luther to reject the idea of torments for the saints: "It is enough for us to know that souls do not leave their bodies to be threatened by the torments and punishments of hell, but enter a prepared bedchamber in which they sleep in peace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> He also rejected the existence of <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">Purgatory</a>, which involved Christian souls undergoing penitential suffering after death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> He affirmed the continuity of one's personal identity beyond death. In his <a href="/wiki/Smalcald_Articles" title="Smalcald Articles">Smalcald Articles</a>, he described the saints as currently residing "in their graves and in heaven."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Lutheran theologian <a href="/wiki/Franz_Pieper" title="Franz Pieper">Franz Pieper</a> observed that Luther's teaching about the state of the Christian's soul after death differed from the later Lutheran theologians such as <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gerhard" title="Johann Gerhard">Johann Gerhard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gotthold_Ephraim_Lessing" title="Gotthold Ephraim Lessing">Lessing</a> (1755) had earlier reached the same conclusion in his analysis of <a href="/wiki/Lutheran_orthodoxy" title="Lutheran orthodoxy">Lutheran orthodoxy</a> on this issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther's <i>Commentary on Genesis</i> contains a passage which concludes that "the soul does not sleep (<i>anima non sic dormit</i>), but wakes (<i>sed vigilat</i>) and experiences visions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francis_Blackburne_(archdeacon)" title="Francis Blackburne (archdeacon)">Francis Blackburne</a> in 1765 argued that <a href="/wiki/John_Jortin" title="John Jortin">John Jortin</a> misread this and other passages from Luther,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Fritschel" title="Gottfried Fritschel">Gottfried Fritschel</a> pointed out in 1867 that it actually refers to the soul of a man "in this life" (<i>homo enim in hac vita</i>) tired from his daily labour (<i>defatigus diurno labore</i>) who at night enters his bedchamber (<i>sub noctem intrat in cubiculum suum</i>) and whose sleep is interrupted by dreams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>Henry Eyster Jacobs' English translation from 1898 reads:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"Nevertheless, the sleep of this life and that of the future life differ; for <b>in this life</b>, man, fatigued by his daily labour, at nightfall goes to his couch, as in peace, to sleep there, and enjoys rest; nor does he know anything of evil, whether of fire or of murder."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sacramentarian_controversy_and_the_Marburg_Colloquy">Sacramentarian controversy and the Marburg Colloquy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/The_Sacrament_of_the_Body_and_Blood_of_Christ%E2%80%94Against_the_Fanatics" title="The Sacrament of the Body and Blood of Christ—Against the Fanatics">The Sacrament of the Body and Blood of Christ—Against the Fanatics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Martin_Luther,_St._Mary%27s_Church,_Mitte,_Berlin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1141" data-file-width="769" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Statue_of_Martin_Luther%2C_St._Mary%27s_Church%2C_Mitte%2C_Berlin.jpg/170px-Statue_of_Martin_Luther%2C_St._Mary%27s_Church%2C_Mitte%2C_Berlin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Statue_of_Martin_Luther%2C_St._Mary%27s_Church%2C_Mitte%2C_Berlin.jpg/255px-Statue_of_Martin_Luther%2C_St._Mary%27s_Church%2C_Mitte%2C_Berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Statue_of_Martin_Luther%2C_St._Mary%27s_Church%2C_Mitte%2C_Berlin.jpg/340px-Statue_of_Martin_Luther%2C_St._Mary%27s_Church%2C_Mitte%2C_Berlin.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Martin_Luther,_St._Mary%27s_Church,_Mitte,_Berlin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue of Martin Luther outside <a href="/wiki/St._Mary%27s_Church,_Berlin" title="St. Mary's Church, Berlin">St. Mary's Church, Berlin</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In October 1529, <a href="/wiki/Philip_I,_Landgrave_of_Hesse" title="Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse">Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse</a>, convoked an assembly of German and Swiss theologians at the <a href="/wiki/Marburg_Colloquy" title="Marburg Colloquy">Marburg Colloquy</a>, to establish doctrinal unity in the emerging Protestant states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Agreement was achieved on fourteen points out of fifteen, the exception being the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> – the <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacrament</a> of the Lord's Supper—an issue crucial to Luther.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>The theologians, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zwingli" title="Zwingli">Zwingli</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melanchthon" title="Melanchthon">Melanchthon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bucer" title="Martin Bucer">Martin Bucer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Oecolampadius" title="Johannes Oecolampadius">Johannes Oecolampadius</a>, differed on the significance of the words spoken by Jesus at the <a href="/wiki/Last_Supper" title="Last Supper">Last Supper</a>: "This is my body which is for you" and "This cup is the new covenant in my blood" (<a href="/wiki/1_Corinthians_11" title="1 Corinthians 11">1 Corinthians 11</a>:23–26).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Luther insisted on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_Presence" title="Real Presence">Real Presence</a> of the body and blood of Christ in the consecrated bread and wine, which he called the <a href="/wiki/Sacramental_union" title="Sacramental union">sacramental union</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> while his opponents believed God to be only spiritually or symbolically present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>Zwingli, for example, denied Jesus' ability to be in more than one place at a time. Luther stressed the <a href="/wiki/Omnipresence" title="Omnipresence">omnipresence</a> of Jesus' human nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> According to transcripts, the debate sometimes became confrontational. Citing Jesus' words "The flesh profiteth nothing" (<a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">John</a> 6.63), Zwingli said, "This passage breaks your neck". "Don't be too proud," Luther retorted, "German necks don't break that easily. This is Hesse, not Switzerland."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> On his table Luther wrote the words "<i>Hoc est corpus meum</i>" ("This is my body") in chalk, to continually indicate his firm stance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the disagreements on the Eucharist, the Marburg Colloquy paved the way for the signing in 1530 of the <a href="/wiki/Augsburg_Confession" title="Augsburg Confession">Augsburg Confession</a>, and for the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Schmalkaldic_League" title="Schmalkaldic League">Schmalkaldic League</a> the following year by leading Protestant nobles such as <a href="/wiki/John_of_Saxony" title="John of Saxony">John of Saxony</a>, Philip of Hesse, and <a href="/wiki/George,_Margrave_of_Brandenburg-Ansbach" title="George, Margrave of Brandenburg-Ansbach">George, Margrave of Brandenburg-Ansbach</a>. The Swiss cities, however, did not sign these agreements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span></h2>
<p>Some scholars have asserted that Luther taught that faith and reason were antithetical in the sense that questions of faith could not be illuminated by reason. He wrote, "All the articles of our Christian faith, which God has revealed to us in His Word, are in presence of reason sheerly impossible, absurd, and false."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> and "[That] Reason in no way contributes to faith. [...] For reason is the greatest enemy that faith has; it never comes to the aid of spiritual things."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> However, though seemingly contradictorily, he also wrote in the latter work that human reason "strives not against faith, when enlightened, but rather furthers and advances it",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> bringing claims he was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fideist" title="Fideist">fideist</a> into dispute. Contemporary Lutheran scholarship, however, has found a different reality in Luther. Luther rather seeks to separate <a href="/wiki/Faith_and_rationality" title="Faith and rationality">faith and reason</a> in order to honor the separate spheres of knowledge that each applies to.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Islam">On Islam</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_and_Islam" title="Protestantism and Islam">Protestantism and Islam</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OsmanenDeutscheKavallerie-1-.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="721" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/OsmanenDeutscheKavallerie-1-.jpg/220px-OsmanenDeutscheKavallerie-1-.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/OsmanenDeutscheKavallerie-1-.jpg/330px-OsmanenDeutscheKavallerie-1-.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/OsmanenDeutscheKavallerie-1-.jpg/440px-OsmanenDeutscheKavallerie-1-.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OsmanenDeutscheKavallerie-1-.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The battle between the Turks and the Christians, in the 16th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the time of the Marburg Colloquy, <a href="/wiki/Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="Suleiman the Magnificent">Suleiman the Magnificent</a> was <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vienna" title="Siege of Vienna">besieging Vienna</a> with a vast <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottoman</a> army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Luther had argued against resisting the Turks in his 1518 <i>Explanation of the Ninety-five Theses</i>, provoking accusations of defeatism. He saw the Turks as a <a href="/wiki/Scourge" title="Scourge">scourge</a> sent by God to punish Christians, as agents of the Biblical <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse" title="Apocalypse">apocalypse</a> that would destroy the <a href="/wiki/Antichrist" title="Antichrist">antichrist</a>, whom Luther believed to be the papacy, and the Roman Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> He consistently rejected the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Religious_war" title="Religious war">Holy War</a>, "as though our people were an army of Christians against the Turks, who were enemies of Christ. This is absolutely contrary to Christ's doctrine and name".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> On the other hand, in keeping with his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_the_two_kingdoms" title="Doctrine of the two kingdoms">doctrine of the two kingdoms</a>, Luther did support non-religious war against the Turks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> In 1526, he argued in <i>Whether Soldiers can be in a State of Grace</i> that national defence is reason for a just war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> By 1529, in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_War_against_the_Turk" title="On War against the Turk">On War against the Turk</a></i>, he was actively urging Emperor <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V</a> and the German people to fight a secular war against the Turks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> He made clear, however, that the spiritual war against an alien faith was separate, to be waged through prayer and repentance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Around the time of the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Vienna" title="Siege of Vienna">Siege of Vienna</a>, Luther wrote a prayer for national deliverance from the Turks, asking God to "give to our emperor perpetual victory over our enemies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1542, Luther read a Latin translation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> He went on to produce several critical pamphlets on Islam, which he called "Mohammedanism" or "the Turk".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Though Luther saw the Muslim faith as a tool of the devil, he was indifferent to its practice: "Let the Turk believe and live as he will, just as one lets the papacy and other false Christians live."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> He opposed banning the publication of the Qur'an, wanting it exposed to scrutiny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antinomian_controversy">Antinomian controversy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LutherPulpit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3883" data-file-width="2003" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/LutherPulpit.jpg/170px-LutherPulpit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/LutherPulpit.jpg/255px-LutherPulpit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/LutherPulpit.jpg/340px-LutherPulpit.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LutherPulpit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pulpit of St. Andreas Church, Eisleben, where Agricola and Luther preached</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Early in 1537, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Agricola" title="Johannes Agricola">Johannes Agricola</a> (1494–1566) – serving at the time as pastor in Luther's birthplace, Eisleben – preached a sermon in which he claimed that God's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_news_(Christianity)" title="Good news (Christianity)">gospel</a>, not God's moral law (the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a>), revealed God's wrath to Christians. Based on this sermon and others by Agricola, Luther suspected that Agricola was behind certain anonymous <a href="/wiki/Antinomianism" title="Antinomianism">antinomian</a> theses circulating in Wittenberg. These theses asserted that the law is no longer to be taught to Christians but belonged only to city hall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> Luther responded to these theses with six series of theses against Agricola and the antinomians, four of which became the basis for <a href="/wiki/Disputation" title="Disputation">disputations</a> between 1538 and 1540.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> He also responded to these assertions in other writings, such as his 1539 <a href="/wiki/Open_letter" title="Open letter">open letter</a> to C. Güttel <i>Against the Antinomians</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> and his book <i>On the Councils and the Church</i> from the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his theses and disputations against the antinomians, Luther reviews and reaffirms, on the one hand, what has been called the "second use of the law," that is, the law as the Holy Spirit's tool to work sorrow over sin in man's heart, thus preparing him for Christ's fulfillment of the law offered in the gospel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> Luther states that everything that is used to work sorrow over sin is called the law, even if it is Christ's life, Christ's death for sin, or God's goodness experienced in creation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> Simply refusing to preach the Ten Commandments among Christians – thereby, as it were, removing the three letters l-a-w from the church – does not eliminate the accusing law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Claiming that the law – in any form – should not be preached to Christians anymore would be tantamount to asserting that Christians are no longer sinners in themselves and that the church consists only of essentially holy people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the other hand, Luther also points out that the Ten Commandments – when considered not as God's condemning judgment but as an expression of his eternal will, that is, of the natural law – also positively teach how the Christian ought to live.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> This has traditionally been called the "third use of the law."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> For Luther, also Christ's life, when understood as an example, is nothing more than an illustration of the Ten Commandments, which a Christian should follow in his or her <a href="/wiki/Vocation" title="Vocation">vocations</a> on a daily basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Ten Commandments, and the beginnings of the renewed life of Christians accorded to them by the sacrament of <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism#Baptism" title="Lutheranism">baptism</a>, are a present foreshadowing of the believers' future <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">angel</a>-like life in heaven in the midst of this life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> Luther's teaching of the Ten Commandments, therefore, has clear <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">eschatological</a> overtones, which, characteristically for Luther, do not encourage world-flight but direct the Christian to service to the neighbor in the common, daily vocations of this perishing world.</p>
<h2><span id="Bigamy_of_Philip_I,_Landgrave_of_Hesse"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bigamy_of_Philip_I.2C_Landgrave_of_Hesse">Bigamy of Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse</span></h2>
<p>From December 1539, Luther became implicated in the <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">bigamy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Philip_I,_Landgrave_of_Hesse" title="Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse">Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse</a>, who wanted to marry one of his wife's ladies-in-waiting. Philip solicited the approval of Luther, Melanchthon, and Bucer, citing as a precedent the polygamy of the patriarchs. The theologians were not prepared to make a general ruling, and they reluctantly advised the <a href="/wiki/Landgrave" title="Landgrave">landgrave</a> that if he was determined, he should marry secretly and keep quiet about the matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> As a result, on 4 March 1540, Philip married a second wife, <a href="/wiki/Margarethe_von_der_Saale" title="Margarethe von der Saale">Margarethe von der Saale</a>, with Melanchthon and Bucer among the witnesses. However, Philip was unable to keep the marriage secret, and he threatened to make Luther's advice public. Luther told him to "tell a good, strong lie" and deny the marriage completely, which Philip did during the subsequent public controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> In the view of Luther's biographer <a href="/wiki/Martin_Brecht" title="Martin Brecht">Martin Brecht</a>, "giving confessional advice for Philip of Hesse was one of the worst mistakes Luther made, and, next to the landgrave himself, who was directly responsible for it, history chiefly holds Luther accountable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Brecht argues that Luther's mistake was not that he gave private pastoral advice, but that he miscalculated the political implications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> The affair caused lasting damage to Luther's reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antisemitism">Antisemitism</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_and_antisemitism" title="Martin Luther and antisemitism">Martin Luther and antisemitism</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_antisemitism" title="Christianity and antisemitism">Christianity and antisemitism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="340" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg/170px-1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg/255px-1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The original title page of <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies" title="On the Jews and Their Lies">On the Jews and Their Lies</a></i>, written by Martin Luther in 1543</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Luther wrote negatively about the Jews throughout his career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-203">[203]</a></sup> Though Luther rarely encountered Jews during his life, his attitudes reflected a theological and cultural tradition which saw Jews as a rejected people guilty of the murder of Christ, and he lived in a locality which had expelled Jews some ninety years earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> He considered the Jews blasphemers and liars because they rejected the divinity of Jesus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> In 1523, Luther advised kindness toward the Jews in <i>That Jesus Christ was Born a Jew</i> and also aimed to convert them to Christianity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> When his efforts at conversion failed, he grew increasingly bitter toward them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther's major works on the Jews were his 60,000-word treatise <i>Von den Juden und Ihren Lügen</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies" title="On the Jews and Their Lies">On the Jews and Their Lies</a></i>), and <i>Vom Schem Hamphoras und vom Geschlecht Christi</i> (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=On_the_Holy_Name_and_the_Lineage_of_Christ&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="On the Holy Name and the Lineage of Christ (page does not exist)">On the Holy Name and the Lineage of Christ</a></i>), both published in 1543, three years before his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Luther argued that the Jews were no longer the chosen people but "the devil's people", and referred to them with violent language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> Citing Deuteronomy 13, wherein <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> commands the killing of idolaters and the burning of their cities and property as an offering to God, Luther called for a "<i>scharfe Barmherzigkeit</i>" ("sharp mercy") against the Jews "to see whether we might save at least a few from the glowing flames."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gritsch8687_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-gritsch8687-211">[211]</a></sup> Luther advocated setting <a href="/wiki/Synagogue" title="Synagogue">synagogues</a> on fire, destroying Jewish <a href="/wiki/Siddur" title="Siddur">prayerbooks</a>, forbidding <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbis</a> from preaching, seizing Jews' property and money, and smashing up their homes, so that these "envenomed worms" would be forced into labour or expelled "for all time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-212">[212]</a></sup> In <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Michael_(historian)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Robert Michael (historian) (page does not exist)">Robert Michael</a>'s view, Luther's words "We are at fault in not slaying them" amounted to a sanction for murder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> "God's anger with them is so intense," Luther concluded, "that gentle mercy will only tend to make them worse, while sharp mercy will reform them but little. Therefore, in any case, away with them!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gritsch8687_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-gritsch8687-211">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther spoke out against the Jews in Saxony, Brandenburg, and Silesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael:Josel.2FStrasbourg_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michael:Josel.2FStrasbourg-214">[214]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Josel_of_Rosheim" title="Josel of Rosheim">Josel of Rosheim</a>, the Jewish spokesman who tried to help the Jews of Saxony in 1537, later blamed their plight on "that priest whose name was Martin Luther—may his body and soul be bound up in hell!—who wrote and issued many heretical books in which he said that whoever would help the Jews was doomed to perdition."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> Josel asked the city of Strasbourg to forbid the sale of Luther's anti-Jewish works: they refused initially, but did so when a Lutheran pastor in <a href="/wiki/Hochfelden,_Bas-Rhin" title="Hochfelden, Bas-Rhin">Hochfelden</a> used a sermon to urge his parishioners to murder Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael:Josel.2FStrasbourg_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-Michael:Josel.2FStrasbourg-214">[214]</a></sup> Luther's influence persisted after his death. Throughout the 1580s, riots led to the expulsion of Jews from several German Lutheran states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther was the most widely read author of his generation, and within Germany he acquired the status of a prophet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> According to the prevailing view among historians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallman1_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wallman1-218">[218]</a></sup> his anti-Jewish rhetoric contributed significantly to the development of antisemitism in Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-219">[219]</a></sup> and in the 1930s and 1940s provided an "ideal underpinning" for the Nazis' attacks on Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> Reinhold Lewin writes that anybody who "wrote against the Jews for whatever reason believed he had the right to justify himself by triumphantly referring to Luther." According to Michael, just about every anti-Jewish book printed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Reich" title="Third Reich">Third Reich</a> contained references to and quotations from Luther. <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">Heinrich Himmler</a> (albeit never a Lutheran, having been brought up Catholic) wrote admiringly of his writings and sermons on the Jews in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> The city of Nuremberg presented a first edition of <i>On the Jews and their Lies</i> to <a href="/wiki/Julius_Streicher" title="Julius Streicher">Julius Streicher</a>, editor of the Nazi newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Der_St%C3%BCrmer" title="Der Stürmer">Der Stürmer</a></i>, on his birthday in 1937; the newspaper described it as the most radically anti-Semitic tract ever published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> It was publicly exhibited in a glass case at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuremberg_rallies" title="Nuremberg rallies">Nuremberg rallies</a> and quoted in a 54-page explanation of the Aryan Law by Dr. E.H. Schulz and Dr. R. Frercks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 17 December 1941, seven Protestant regional church confederations issued a statement agreeing with the policy of forcing Jews to wear the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_badge" title="Yellow badge">yellow badge</a>, "since after his bitter experience Luther had already suggested preventive measures against the Jews and their expulsion from German territory." According to <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Goldhagen" title="Daniel Goldhagen">Daniel Goldhagen</a>, Bishop <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Martin_Sasse&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Martin Sasse (page does not exist)">Martin Sasse</a>, a leading Protestant churchman, published a compendium of Luther's writings shortly after <a href="/wiki/Kristallnacht" title="Kristallnacht">Kristallnacht</a>, for which <a href="/wiki/Diarmaid_MacCulloch" title="Diarmaid MacCulloch">Diarmaid MacCulloch</a>, Professor of the History of the Church in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> argued that Luther's writing was a "blueprint."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> Sasse applauded the burning of the synagogues and the coincidence of the day, writing in the introduction, "On 10 November 1938, on Luther's birthday, the synagogues are burning in Germany." The German people, he urged, ought to heed these words "of the greatest antisemite of his time, the warner of his people against the Jews."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:25%; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>"There is a world of difference between his belief in salvation and a racial ideology. Nevertheless, his misguided agitation had the evil result that Luther fatefully became one of the 'church fathers' of anti-Semitism and thus provided material for the modern hatred of the Jews, cloaking it with the authority of the Reformer."</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;"><a href="/wiki/Martin_Brecht" title="Martin Brecht">Martin Brecht</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>At the heart of scholars' debate about Luther's influence is whether it is <a href="/wiki/Anachronism" title="Anachronism">anachronistic</a> to view his work as a precursor of the racial antisemitism of the Nazis. Some scholars see Luther's influence as limited, and the Nazis' use of his work as opportunistic. <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Wallmann_(theologian)" title="Johannes Wallmann (theologian)">Johannes Wallmann</a> argues that Luther's writings against the Jews were largely ignored in the 18th and 19th centuries, and that there was no continuity between Luther's thought and Nazi ideology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Uwe_Siemon-Netto" title="Uwe Siemon-Netto">Uwe Siemon-Netto</a> agreed, arguing that it was because the Nazis were already anti-Semites that they revived Luther's work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SiemonNetto2_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-SiemonNetto2-229">[229]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hans_J._Hillerbrand&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hans J. Hillerbrand (page does not exist)">Hans J. Hillerbrand</a> agreed that to focus on Luther was to adopt an essentially ahistorical perspective of Nazi antisemitism that ignored other contributory factors in <a href="/wiki/History_of_Germany" title="History of Germany">German history</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HillerbrandEB_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-HillerbrandEB-230">[230]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Roland_Bainton" title="Roland Bainton">Roland Bainton</a>, noted church historian and Luther biographer, wrote "One could wish that Luther had died before ever [<i>On the Jews and Their Lies</i>] was written. His position was entirely religious and in no respect racial."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> However, Christopher J. Probst, in his book <i>Demonizing the Jews: Luther and the Protestant Church in Nazi Germany</i> (2012), shows that a large number of German Lutheran clergy and theologians during the Nazi Third Reich used Luther's hostile publications towards the Jews and their Jewish religion to justify at least in part the anti-Semitic policies of the National Socialists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some scholars, such as Mark U. Edwards in his book <i>Luther's Last Battles: Politics and Polemics 1531–46</i> (1983), suggest that since Luther's increasingly antisemitic views developed during the years his health deteriorated, it is possible they were at least partly the product of a state of mind. Edwards also comments that Luther often deliberately used "vulgarity and violence" for effect, both in his writings condemning the Jews and in diatribes against "Turks" (Muslims) and Catholics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the 1980s, Lutheran denominations have repudiated Martin Luther's statements against the Jews and have rejected the use of them to incite hatred against Lutherans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> Strommen et al.'s 1970 survey of 4,745 North American Lutherans aged 15–65 found that, compared to the other minority groups under consideration, Lutherans were the least prejudiced toward Jews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Professor Richard (Dick) Geary, former Professor of Modern History at the University of Nottingham, England, and the author of <i>Hitler and Nazism</i> (Routledge 1993), published an article in the magazine <i><a href="/wiki/History_Today" title="History Today">History Today</a></i> examining electoral trends in Weimar Germany between 1928 and 1933. Geary noted, based on his research, that the Nazi Party received disproportionately more votes from Protestant than Catholic areas of Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Final_years,_illness_and_death"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years.2C_illness_and_death">Final years, illness and death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2216" data-file-width="1616" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg/220px-DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg/330px-DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg/440px-DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Luther on his deathbed, painting by <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">Lucas Cranach the Elder</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Luther had been suffering from ill health for years, including <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9ni%C3%A8re%27s_disease" title="Ménière's disease">Ménière's disease</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertigo_(medical)" title="Vertigo (medical)">vertigo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fainting" title="Fainting">fainting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tinnitus" title="Tinnitus">tinnitus</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataract</a> in one eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9019884_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9019884-240">[240]</a></sup> From 1531 to 1546 his health deteriorated further. The years of struggle with Rome, the antagonisms with and among his fellow reformers, and the scandal that ensued from the <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">bigamy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Philip_I,_Landgrave_of_Hesse" title="Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse">Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse</a> incident, in which Luther had played a leading role, all may have contributed. In 1536, he began to suffer from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kidney_stone" title="Kidney stone">kidney and bladder stones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">arthritis</a>, and an ear infection ruptured an ear drum. In December 1544, he began to feel the effects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angina_pectoris" title="Angina pectoris">angina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup></p>
<p>His poor physical health made him short-tempered and even harsher in his writings and comments. His wife Katharina was overheard saying, "Dear husband, you are too rude," and he responded, "They are teaching me to be rude."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> In 1545 and 1546 Luther preached three times in the <a href="/wiki/Marktkirche_Unser_Lieben_Frauen" title="Marktkirche Unser Lieben Frauen">Market Church</a> in Halle, staying with his friend <a href="/wiki/Justus_Jonas" title="Justus Jonas">Justus Jonas</a> during <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>His last sermon was delivered at <a href="/wiki/Eisleben" title="Eisleben">Eisleben</a>, his place of birth, on 15 February 1546, three days before his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> It was "entirely devoted to the obdurate Jews, whom it was a matter of great urgency to expel from all German territory," according to <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Poliakov" title="Léon Poliakov">Léon Poliakov</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> James Mackinnon writes that it concluded with a "fiery summons to drive the Jews bag and baggage from their midst, unless they desisted from their calumny and their usury and became Christians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> Luther said, "we want to practice Christian love toward them and pray that they convert," but also that they are "our public enemies ... and if they could kill us all, they would gladly do so. And so often they do."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup></p>
<p>Luther's final journey, to Mansfeld, was taken because of his concern for his siblings' families continuing in their father Hans Luther's copper mining trade. Their livelihood was threatened by Count Albrecht of Mansfeld bringing the industry under his own control. The controversy that ensued involved all four Mansfeld counts: Albrecht, Philip, John George, and Gerhard. Luther journeyed to Mansfeld twice in late 1545 to participate in the negotiations for a settlement, and a third visit was needed in early 1546 for their completion.</p>
<p>The negotiations were successfully concluded on 17 February 1546. After 8 a.m., he experienced chest pains. When he went to his bed, he prayed, "Into your hand I commit my spirit; you have redeemed me, O Lord, faithful God" (Ps. 31:5), the common prayer of the dying. At 1 a.m. he awoke with more chest pain and was warmed with hot towels. He thanked God for revealing his Son to him in whom he had believed. His companions, Justus Jonas and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Michael_Coelius&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Michael Coelius (page does not exist)">Michael Coelius</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> (<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_Coelius" title="de:Michael Coelius">de</a>)</span>, shouted loudly, "Reverend father, are you ready to die trusting in your Lord Jesus Christ and to confess the doctrine which you have taught in his name?" A distinct "Yes" was Luther's reply.</p>
<p>An apoplectic stroke deprived him of his speech, and he died shortly afterwards at 2:45 a.m. on 18 February 1546, aged 62, in Eisleben, the city of his birth. He was buried in the <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Church,_Wittenberg" title="All Saints' Church, Wittenberg">Castle Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wittenberg" title="Wittenberg">Wittenberg</a>, beneath the pulpit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> The funeral was held by his friends <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Bugenhagen" title="Johannes Bugenhagen">Johannes Bugenhagen</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacKim19_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacKim19-249">[249]</a></sup> A year later, troops of Luther's adversary <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor</a> entered the town, but were ordered by Charles not to disturb the grave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacKim19_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacKim19-249">[249]</a></sup></p>
<p>A piece of paper was later found on which Luther had written his last statement. The statement was in Latin, apart from "We are beggars," which was in German. The statement reads:</p>
<blockquote>
<div class="poem">
<ol>
<li>No one can understand <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bucolics" title="Bucolics">Bucolics</a></i> unless he has been a shepherd for five years. No one can understand Virgil's <i><a href="/wiki/Georgics" title="Georgics">Georgics</a></i>, unless he has been a farmer for five years.<br/></li>
<li>No one can understand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cicero%27s_Letters" title="Cicero's Letters">Cicero's <i>Letters</i></a> (or so I teach), unless he has busied himself in the affairs of some prominent state for twenty years.<br/></li>
<li>Know that no one can have indulged in the Holy Writers sufficiently, unless he has governed churches for a hundred years with the prophets, such as <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elisha" title="Elisha">Elisha</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a>, Christ and the apostles.<br/></li>
</ol>
<p><br/>
Do not assail this divine <a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a>; nay, rather prostrate revere the ground that it treads.<br/>
<br/>
We are beggars: this is true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</blockquote>
<p>The tomb of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" title="Philipp Melanchthon">Philipp Melanchthon</a>, Luther's contemporary and fellow reformer, is also located in the All Saints' Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
